                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adfm.202414041')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="0cf7182b229b472a9c7109b010ea1c5b"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="0cf7182b229b472a9c7109b010ea1c5b">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '695f34fa-ccac-4447-a790-606d159981db'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://advanced.onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '7663F5EE9FDBDD7DFC182D67A4D01999';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d96921816" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Interface stability is a key to practical applications of high-rate sodium metal batteries (SMBs). The general sodium metal anode (SMA), for example, suffers from an unstable solid electrolyte interface (SEI), which may induce severe dendrite growth and continuous Na consumption, particularly under high rates. At the same time, the cathode electrolyte interface (CEI) of a widely adopted cathode material Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) has to tolerate the massive volume changes during the rapid Na<sup>+</sup> ion intercalation/extraction, resulting in structural degradations and the capacity loss. Here, a facile strategy is reported by introducing aluminum ethoxide to a conventional ether-based electrolyte, to promote the formation of an alumina-rich SEI and CEI, which facilitates the rapid Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport across the interface and promotes the uniform Na deposition. The resultant Na || Na symmetric cell demonstrates a stable cycling of 4800&nbsp;h at 10&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, and a Na || NVP full cell exhibits a high capacity retention of 81.28% after 6000 cycles at 40C. The simultaneous interface engineering of both the Na metal anode and the NVP cathode via the facile organic aluminum additive opens up a new avenue toward practical application of high-rate SMBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202414041-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202414041-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The growing demand for smart grid, electrical vehicles (EVs), and portable electronics, has spurred global interest in rechargeable batteries with high energy density, low-cost, excellent rate capability, and cycle stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0001_R_d96921805e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy Nat. Commun. Nano Res. Energy Tungsten Nano Res. Energy">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The lithium-ion battery (LIB) could fulfill this demand to a large extent, and it has been under the spotlight for broad applications since its first commercialization in 1991.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0002_R_d96921805e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Rev. Mater. Adv. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the uneven global distribution of limited lithium resources and the escalating lithium prices have shadowed LIB's prospects for widespread and cost-effective applications in the future.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0003_R_d96921805e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this case, the widely-distributed cost-effective sodium salts with acceptable electrochemical performance makes the sodium battery a promising alternative to power EVs and beyond. Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted intense investigations since the demonstration of sodium-intercalated hard carbon anode in 2000,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0004_R_d96921805e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - J. Electrochem. Soc. Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> however, the energy density of SIBs is typically within 160&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0005_R_d96921805e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Energy">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> only about half that of advanced commercial LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0006_R_d96921805e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A promising way to increase the energy density is to adopt the Na metal anode, which has a low standard potential of −2.714&nbsp;V (versus the standard hydrogen electrode), and a high theoretical specific capacity of 1165 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0007_R_d96921805e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Mater. Chem. Soc. Rev. Energy Storage Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Nevertheless, the practical application of sodium metal anodes (SMAs) has been impeded by several challenges, including the unstable solid electrolyte interphase&nbsp;(SEI), unrestricted dendrite growth, and electrolyte degradation, which can be severely aggravated under high current densities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0003_R_d96921805e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Owing to the strong electropositivity, Na can easily react with most organic electrolytes, leading to the SEI formation<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0002_R_d96921805e583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Rev. Mater. Adv. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and continuous consumption of the electrolyte. Besides, the uneven Na electrodeposition tends to induce the dendrite growth,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0001_R_d96921805e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy Nat. Commun. Nano Res. Energy Tungsten Nano Res. Energy">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which may further result in short circuits, poor cycle life, thermal runaway, or even explosion. While for the cathode, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) is widely employed in SMBs due to its excellent structural integrity and thermal stability;<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0008_R_d96921805e603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Mater. Chem. A ACS Nano">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> however, NVP has a poor electrical conductivity,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0009_R_d96921805e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Power Sources">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the rapid intercalation/extraction of large Na<sup>+</sup> ions at high rates induces&nbsp;severe volumetric alterations,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0010_R_d96921805e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which can lead to rapid&nbsp;capacity loss.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0011_R_d96921805e621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Power Sources">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Constructing robust interfaces at both electrodes to promote a uniform Na ion flux, suppressing the Na dendrite growth, and shielding the active material from continuous side reactions with the electrolyte, are of great significance to realize high-rate SMBs. Typical strategies include, introducing proper electrolyte additives,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0012_R_d96921805e628" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> solvents,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0013_R_d96921805e633" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> employing high-concentration,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0014_R_d96921805e638" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Power Sources">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> or localized high-concentration electrolytes,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0015_R_d96921805e644" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Energy Lett.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> building protective coatings on SMA surface,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0002_R_d96921805e649" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Rev. Mater. Adv. Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0016_R_d96921805e649" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Lett.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc. Generally, conventional O-containing organics formed in SEI are amorphous and tend to undergo swelling or dissolution in electrolyte solvents,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0017_R_d96921805e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Joule">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and their low ionic conductivities may hinder the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions across the interface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0018_R_d96921805e662" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nat. Energy">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, inorganic-rich SEI exhibits enhanced physicochemical properties, including a higher ionic conductivity, enhanced mechanical strength, and increased surface energy, which is advantageous to enable the uniform and rapid diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions along with remarkably reduced side reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0012_R_d96921805e670" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0019_R_d96921805e670" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among various inorganic SEI components, NaF has been widely explored to provide a strong mechanical strength to suppress the dendrite growth and promote the uniform Na deposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0012_R_d96921805e675" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0020_R_d96921805e675" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Energy Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the low ionic conductivity<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0021_R_d96921805e680" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> results in the undesired increase of the interfacial impedance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0012_R_d96921805e686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0022_R_d96921805e686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Research">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, the coating of a thin layer of Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> on the SMA surface, via atomic layer deposition (ALD),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0002_R_d96921805e695" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Rev. Mater. Adv. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> molecular layer deposition (MLD),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0016_R_d96921805e701" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Lett.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> or plasma-enhanced ALD (PEALD),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0016_R_d96921805e706" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Lett.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> could protect the SMA against side reactions with the electrolyte, while working as an excellent Na<sup>+</sup> ion conductor<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0023_R_d96921805e714" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Small">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> to promote the uniform Na deposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0024_R_d96921805e719" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Commun.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, these Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> deposition methods suffer from high costs, dedicated operations, and poor scalability for volume production.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we report a facile strategy to simultaneously construct an uniform alumina-rich SEI and CEI for high rate SMBs via introducing aluminum ethoxide (Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub>) additive into a NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>-containing ether-based electrolyte. Besides the advantages brought by the alumina coating discussed above, we further found that the addition of Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> could tune the solvation structure of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the electrolyte to suppress the dendrite formation (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0001">1a</a>) from resultant SMAs and enable their stable cycling under high rates. Moreover, the simultaneous formation of an alumina-containing CEI further mitigates the effects of structural degradation of the cathode material during cycling. As a result, the symmetric Na || Na cell exhibits a remarkable ultra-long cycle life of over 5300 and 4800&nbsp;h at 5 and 10&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, respectively, while a Na || NVP full cell retains 83.46% of its initial capacity after 4000 cycles at 20C, with a high average Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 99.46%. The stable operation can also be extended to an extremely high rate of 40C with ultralong cyclability of 6000 cycles. This work provides a facile, eco-friendly, and efficient avenue to engineer the electrode interfaces for high-rate and long-lasting SMBs.</p>

            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414041-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4a3ed559-16be-47b0-821b-0732052c50d8/adfm202414041-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>

      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202414041-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202414041-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202414041-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202414041-sec-0030-title">2.1 Physicochemical Characterization of the Formulated Electrolytes</h3>
            
            <p>To evaluate the role of Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> addition to the electrolyte, the physicochemical properties of the electrolyte were characterized. We used 1&nbsp;M NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> (NaOTf) in tetraglyme (G4) as the baseline electrolyte (denoted as NG4), and we added 1.5&nbsp;wt.% (100&nbsp;m<span class="smallCaps">m</span>) Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> into NG4 as a newly formulated electrolyte (denoted as AE100), and we further prepared 100&nbsp;m<span class="smallCaps">m</span> Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> in G4 (denoted as AEG4) as a control solution. Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR), Raman spectroscopy, and nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectroscopy were performed to explore the solvation structure of the electrolyte.</p>
            
            <p>The Raman spectra of these four solutions are compared in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0001">1b</a>, and the broad peak at 850&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> can be attributed to the vibration of G4 solvent molecules;<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0025_R_d96921805e812" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> this peak shifted to 868&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> in NG4 and AE100, due to the interaction between G4 and Na<sup>+</sup>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0026_R_d96921805e822" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which is further corroborated by the disappearance of the peak from free G4 molecules in the deconvoluted Raman spectra at 2986&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). FTIR spectra are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0001">1c</a>, the peak at 1112&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> in G4 originates from the stretching vibration of C─O─C, and it undergoes a red shift to 1105&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> in NG4, due to the weakened C─O bonding from the Na<sup>+</sup> ion effect.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0027_R_d96921805e842" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Energy Storage Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks observed at 754&nbsp; and 1225&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> stem from the symmetric stretching of ─CF<sub>3</sub> in free OTf<sup>−</sup> ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0028_R_d96921805e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Ionics">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the intensity of the peak at 754&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> in NG4 is noticeably weaker than that in AE100, indicating an increased abundance of free OTf<sup>−</sup> ions in AE100, which is consistent with AE100's significantly stronger Raman intensity of free OTf<sup>−</sup> ions at 755 and 1034&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0001">1b</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0029_R_d96921805e871" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nano-Micro Lett.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peak at 639&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0001">1d</a> is attributed to the symmetric vibration of ─SO<sub>3</sub> from NaOTf,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0030_R_d96921805e884" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Eur. Polym. J.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with an intensity that is stronger in NG4 than in AE100, due to the increased presence of free OTf<sup>−</sup> anions in NG4.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0031_R_d96921805e892" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Spectrochim. Acta A">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Encouragingly, a new peak emerged at 1745&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> in AE100, which may be attributed to the synergistic interaction between Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> and NaOTf, suggesting that the introduction of Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> may have a beneficial effect on the solvation sheath of Na<sup>+</sup> ions and contribute to the regulation of the electrode/electrolyte interface.</p>
            
            <p>To further assess the impact of Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> on the Na<sup>+</sup> ion solvation structure, we performed the classical molecular dynamics (MD) simulation. As revealed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), the incorporation of Al<sup>3+</sup> ions slightly squeeze out G4 molecules while increasing the presence of OTf<sup>−</sup> within the primary solvation sheath of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, which may potentially promote the formation of an inorganic-rich SEI to stabilize the Na metal anode. By calculating the electrostatic potential (ESP) of G4, coordinated Na<sup>+</sup> with G4 (denoted as Na-G4), and coordinated Al<sup>3+</sup> with G4 (denoted as Al-G4), as well as the binding energies of the corresponding processes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), it is revealed that G4 solvent molecules exhibit the lowest ESP and binding energy (−734.94&nbsp;kcal mol<sup>−1</sup>) when coordinated with Al<sup>3+</sup>. The binding energy of Al<sup>3+</sup> to solvated G4 within the Na-G4 cluster is relatively low (−644.49&nbsp;kcal mol<sup>−1</sup>), indicating that Al<sup>3+</sup> ions prefer to bind with free G4 molecules rather than extracting solvated G4 molecules from Na-G4.</p>
            
            <p>Solution NMR was conducted to further explore the effect of Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> on the solvation structure. Overall, the chemical shifts of AE100 electrolyte in <sup>1</sup>H NMR (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) and <sup>23</sup>Na NMR (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0001">1d</a>) spectra exhibit a slightly downfield shift compared with that of the NG4 electrolyte; the de-shielding effect may indicate that G4 molecules have entered the solvation sheath of Al<sup>3+</sup> ions and present a strong dipole interaction,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0032_R_d96921805e961" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which result in the decrease of the electron cloud density of Na<sup>+</sup> ions and H atoms from G4 molecules.</p>
            
            <p>A series of characterizations were performed to evaluate the effect of the Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> additive on electrochemical properties of the newly formulated electrolyte. Linear sweep voltammetry (LSV) data in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0001">1e</a> reveal that, the introduction of Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> additive is beneficial to enhance the electrochemical stability of the baseline electrolyte, with the onset oxidation potential increased from 4.15 to 4.30&nbsp;V. While the cyclic voltammetry (CV) profiles in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a</a> (Supporting Information) show that the Na || Cu cell exhibits a lower initial Na plating overpotential of 24.7&nbsp;mV in AE100 than 51.1&nbsp;mV in NG4, implying an enhanced ionic conductivity of the SEI formed in AE100,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0033_R_d96921805e982" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which is consistent with the reduced nucleation overpotential of 58.2&nbsp;mV in AE100 from the 72.6&nbsp;mV in NG4 during the Na electrodeposition on the Cu substrate (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, the Tafel plots shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information) reveals that, the exchange current density in AE100 is about twice that in NG4 (4.85 vs 2.46&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>), indicating the enhanced charge transfer kinetics at the interface<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0034_R_d96921805e996" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nat. Energy">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> in AE100.</p>
            
            <p>Afterward, we evaluated the ionic conductivity of the two electrolytes by the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) with stainless steel || stainless steel symmetric cells. As revealed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0001">1f</a>, AE100 exhibits an ionic conductivity of 2.67 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>, which is 30% higher than 2.01 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> of NG4. According to the Sand's time model,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0035_R_d96921805e1011" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> a higher Na<sup>+</sup> ionic conductivity is advantageous to delay the Na dendrite growth,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0003_R_d96921805e1019" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> thus prolonging the cycle life of SMBs. Through acquiring the EIS plots at temperatures spanning from 298.15 to 278.15 K and fitted with the equivalent circuit (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information), the temperature-dependence SEI interface impedance (<i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>) and charge transfer impedance (<i>R</i><sub>CT</sub>) were profiled in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0001">1g</a>. The activation energy of the Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport across the SEI (<i>E</i><sub>a1</sub>) and Na<sup>+</sup>-desolvation (<i>E</i><sub>a2</sub>) were then calculated based on the Arrhenius equation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0036_R_d96921805e1048" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Energy Environ. Sci. J. Electrochem. Soc.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In line with the above results, the <i>E</i><sub>a1</sub> for AE100 (25.74&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) is lower than that (26.68&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) for NG4, while <i>E</i><sub>a2</sub> for AE100 (26.76&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) is less than half of that (61.20&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) for NG4 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information), indicating the enhanced interfacial Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics and significantly boosted desolvation process<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0036_R_d96921805e1074" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Energy Environ. Sci. J. Electrochem. Soc.">36</a>, <a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0037_R_d96921805e1074" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Power Sources">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with the introduction of the aluminum ethoxide.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202414041-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202414041-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance in Na || Na Symmetric Cells</h3>
            
            <p>The effect of the Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> additive on the electrochemical performance of Na metal anodes was first tested with Na || Na symmetric cells. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0002">2a,b</a>, the cells with NG4, and NG4 added with 75 and 120&nbsp;m<span class="smallCaps">m</span> Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> (denoted as AE75 and AE120, respectively) electrolytes run into short circuits after being cycled under a current density of 5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> for 0.65, 12.15, and 24.5&nbsp;h, respectively, and the voltage profiles exhibit severe fluctuations. In sharp contrast, the cell loaded with the AE100 electrolyte demonstrates significantly enhanced cycle stability for over 5300&nbsp;h with a low polarization voltage of 218&nbsp;mV, making 100&nbsp;m<span class="smallCaps">m</span> the optimum concentration of the Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> additive in the NG4 electrolyte; the reason might be that, a lower additive concentration might be difficult to form a robust SEI,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0038_R_d96921805e1109" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> while the SEI formed in a high-concentration electrolyte may be not very uniform due to the limited additive solubility and the increased viscosity. When the current density was doubled to 10&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, the Na || Na cell with AE100 electrolyte can cycle stably for 4800&nbsp;h with a low polarization of 283&nbsp;mV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0002">2c</a>), which makes it among the best in long-lasting high-rate SMAs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0002_R_d96921805e1120" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Rev. Mater. Adv. Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0016_R_d96921805e1120" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Lett.">16</a>, <a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0020_R_d96921805e1120" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Energy Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0038_R_d96921805e1120" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">38</a>, <a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0039_R_d96921805e1120" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Commun. Small">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as compared in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414041-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/97d7df26-4bae-4adc-a756-725a872e05e4/adfm202414041-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/97d7df26-4bae-4adc-a756-725a872e05e4/adfm202414041-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e21d9759-da84-4c8c-b0b2-099b54827914/adfm202414041-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/97d7df26-4bae-4adc-a756-725a872e05e4/adfm202414041-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414041-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414041" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The electrochemical performance of Na metal symmetric cells. Electrochemical performance of SMAs in NG4 and AE100 electrolytes. a) Cycle performance of Na || Na symmetric cells in NG4, AE75, AE100, and AE100 electrolytes at 5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. b) The enlarged view of the red dashed area in a), with insets corresponding to the zoom-in views of gray dashed areas in b). c) Voltage-time profile of a Na metal symmetric cell in AE100 at 10&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. EIS data after different cycles of the Na symmetric cell (cycled at 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>) in d) AE100 and e) NG4 electrolyte, with insets the equivalent circuits for fitting.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The EIS characterization was performed to track the evolution of the electrode/electrolyte interfacial impedance (<i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>), which corresponds to the horizontally projected width of the semicircle in each EIS profile. As shown in (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0002">2d,e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information), and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), after the formation cycle, the interfacial impedance of the Na symmetric cell with AE100 gradually stabilizes at ≈3 Ω in a few cycles, while the one with NG4 exhibits a higher value, which might be traced back to the formation of highly ionically conductive Na-Al-O species in the SEI formed in AE100,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0016_R_d96921805e1177" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Lett.">16</a>, <a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0040_R_d96921805e1177" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as to be discussed in detail in the following section. Meanwhile, the value of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> in AE100 is slightly smaller than that of NG4, which is consistent with the above analyses on the reduced Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation energy barrier.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202414041-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202414041-sec-0050-title">2.3 SEI Characterizations and Formation Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) was performed to compare the effect of the Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> additive on the surface morphology of Na metal anodes after cycling under high current densities. Though the surface of a pristine Na metal anode is overall smooth (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information), it becomes very bumpy with plenty of craters after 200 cycles at 5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> in NG4 electrolyte (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0003">3a,b</a>), indicating that the non-uniform Na electrodeposition, which may trigger uncontrolled dendrite growth and continuous consumption of the electrolyte. Comparable effect was found in Na cells cycled at 15&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> in the two electrolytes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). As a result, the cell was shorted in 6 cycles, as indicated by the sharp voltage drop from 153 to 9&nbsp;mV in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0002">2a,b</a>. In comparison, for the symmetric cell in AE100 cycled under the same condition, the Na surface remains overall flat and smooth with nanoscale particles evenly distributed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0003">3c,d</a>), indicating a uniform Na<sup>+</sup> ion flux during the electrodeposition, which allowed the cell to operate stably for over 5000&nbsp;h (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0002">2a</a>). Comparable effect was observed in the Na || Cu half cell testing, further showing the SEI for stable high-rate cycling engineered with the Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> additive in the electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414041-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b34aaed4-cfdc-4e29-95fa-d0581dde53d7/adfm202414041-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b34aaed4-cfdc-4e29-95fa-d0581dde53d7/adfm202414041-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c1d5dcff-6a19-4d8f-b8c8-1f9337b0ed50/adfm202414041-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b34aaed4-cfdc-4e29-95fa-d0581dde53d7/adfm202414041-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414041-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414041" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterization of the cycled Na metal surface and Na deposition behavior. SEM images of the Na metal surface after 200 cycles at 5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> with a, b) NG4 electrolyte, and c, d) AE100 electrolyte, respectively. 2D and 3D AFM images of SEI layer formed on Cu foil with a deposited capacity of 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> at a current density of 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> in e) NG4 and f) AE100. In situ optical microscopy images of Na symmetric cells operated in g) NG4 and h) AE100, respectively. XPS depth-profiling of the Al 2p spectra of Na metal anode after 20 cycles in i) AE100 electrolyte and j) NG4 electrolyte, respectively.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To further compare the surface morphology and mechanical properties of the SEI layer formed in different electrolytes, the atomic force microscopy (AFM) characterization was performed. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0003">3e</a>, the SEI formed in NG4 electrolyte exhibits a roughness of 32.2&nbsp;nm, compared to 22.0&nbsp;nm of the SEI formed in AE100 electrolyte showing a smoother surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0003">3f</a>). What's more, the latter was measured to have a high Young's modulus of 11778&nbsp;MPa, over three times that of 3791&nbsp;MPa in NG4 electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information); such high strength SEI is advantageous to suppress the dendrite growth during the cycle process.</p>
            
            <p>To identify the difference of the Na deposition behavior using the two electrolytes, in situ optical microscopy was used to observe the real-time discharge process of Na || Na symmetric cells. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0003">3g,h</a>, when tested at 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, bubbles, “dead” Na, and dendrites were observed only in the cell using NG4, which indicates the uneven Na<sup>+</sup> ion flux and fragile SEI during the cycling in NG4, leading to the continuous reaction between the electrolyte and Na metal, as well as the unrestricted dendrites growth. These results demonstrate that AE100 electrolyte can be beneficial to form the stable, robust, and highly conductive SEI for uniform Na deposition, which is consistent with the above enhanced electrochemical performance achieved with the AE100 electrolyte.</p>
            
            <p>The X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) with depth profiling was carried out to examine the effect of the Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> additive on the SEI composition after the cycling process. In the high-resolution F 1s spectra of the SEI formed after cycling in the AE100 and NG4 electrolytes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information), the peaks at 684.8 and 688.1&nbsp;eV are assigned to NaF and ─CF<i><sub>x</sub></i>, respectively, which should originate from the reduction of OTf<sup>−</sup> in the electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0012_R_d96921805e1295" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0023_R_d96921805e1295" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Small">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0041_R_d96921805e1295" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With the increasing Ar<sup>+</sup> ion etching time, the ─CF<i><sub>x</sub></i> signal gradually disappears in the SEI formed in both AE100 and NG4 electrolytes, the F content remains relatively stable in the depth direction for the former (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a</a>, Supporting Information), while it decreases to some extent in the latter (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16b</a>, Supporting Information). Such F-rich SEI formed in the AE100 electrolyte tends to promote the uniform Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0020_R_d96921805e1315" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Energy Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the S 2p XPS spectra shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information), the peaks from SO<sub>4</sub><sup>2−</sup>, SO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup>, Na<sub>2</sub>S<i><sub>x</sub></i>, and Na<sub>2</sub>S, are believed to come from the decomposition of OTf<sup>−</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0039_R_d96921805e1339" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Commun. Small">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The overall S content remains relatively low and stable in the depth direction (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information) for both SEIs, these S-containing inorganic species are believed to be beneficial to protect the Na metal surface from side reactions with the electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0038_R_d96921805e1348" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The peak at 72.0&nbsp;eV of the Al 2p XPS spectra in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0003">3i</a> is assigned to Al metal,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0042_R_d96921805e1359" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Power Sources">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> indicating that the additive Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> experiences redox reactions during the cycling process, while the presence of Al─O species detected at 75.0&nbsp;eV suggests the formation of Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> in the SEI formed in AE100 electrolyte,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0024_R_d96921805e1371" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Commun.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which is further confirmed by the appearance of a peak at 530.0&nbsp;eV in the O 1s spectra<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0042_R_d96921805e1376" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Power Sources">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18a</a> (Supporting Information). As the etching proceeds, there is a slight increase in the Al content (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, no Al-containing species has been detected in the Na SEI formed in NG4 electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0003">3j</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18b</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, the C 1s and Na 1s XPS spectra in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information) reveals that, the AE100-derived SEI contains significantly lower level of organic species. These results confirm the crucial role of Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> in the tuning of the SEI composition, to favorably form the composite structure rich in Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>/Al, which plays a pivotal role in promoting the mitigation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions with a higher ionic conductivity<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0024_R_d96921805e1406" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Commun.">24</a>, <a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0043_R_d96921805e1406" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Electrochim. Acta">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and smoother electrodeposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0044_R_d96921805e1411" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - ACS Energy Lett. Adv. Mater.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202414041-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202414041-sec-0060-title">2.4 Full Cell Performance</h3>
            
            <p>In order to evaluate the possibility of using AE100 electrolyte for practical high-rate SMB applications, full cells were assembled with NVP as the cathode material. The overall rate performance is compared in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0004">4a</a>. The full cell with AE100 electrolyte exhibits higher specific discharge capacities than that of the cell based on NG4 electrolyte, and the capacity gap widens as the rate goes up. For example, the full cell with AE100 electrolyte exhibits 101.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5C, over 30% higher than that of 74.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in the full cell with NG4 electrolyte. At 20C, the full cell with AE100 electrolyte still demonstrated a discharge capacity of 60.21 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, far higher than that of the cell in NG4.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414041-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fb7c4359-a0e0-4641-acff-48f14ccad537/adfm202414041-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/fb7c4359-a0e0-4641-acff-48f14ccad537/adfm202414041-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/18e92067-f1fa-4872-874c-88411af262d0/adfm202414041-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fb7c4359-a0e0-4641-acff-48f14ccad537/adfm202414041-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414041-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414041" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The electrochemical performance of Na metal full cells. a) Rate performance of the full cells with the AE100 (green) and NG4 (orange) electrolytes. b) Long-term cycle stability of a Na || NVP full cell at 20C (1C&nbsp;=&nbsp;117.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) with the two electrolytes. The EIS plots of Na metal full cells after different number of cycles with c) AE100 electrolyte, and d) NG4 electrolyte. e) The comparison of the solution resistance (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>) and <i>R</i><sub>interface</sub> acquired in the NG4 and AE100 electrolyte, respectively.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The long-term high-rate cycle stability of full cells with AE100 electrolyte and NG4 electrolyte was tested at rates from 5 to 50C. As compared in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0004">4b</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20–S25</a> (Supporting Information), full cells with AE100 electrolyte demonstrates enhanced cyclability, higher capacity retention, and smaller overpotential at all rates tested. For example, at 20C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0004">4b</a>), the full cell with AE100 electrolyte delivers an initial discharge specific capacity of 60.23 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with 83.46% capacity retention ratio after 4000 cycles, and an average CE of 99.46% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0004">4b</a>); As a sharp contrast, the full cell with NG4 electrolyte retains only 31.73% of its initial capacity (only 49.39 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) after 4000 cycles. The EIS plots in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0004">4c–e</a> shows that, the interfacial impedance of the full cell with AE100 electrolyte remains relatively stable during the cycling process, while that of the full cell with NG4 electrolyte shows a significant increase after 500 cycles; the corresponding voltage profiles in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a> (Supporting Information) reveal an overpotential in the former cell of ≈10% lower than that of the latter. To further test the oxidative stability of the AE100 electrolyte, we used the high-voltage cathode Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F (NVPOF) in full cells. As depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a> (Supporting Information), the AE100-based NVPOF full cell exhibits an initial discharge capacity of 114.52 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20C, along with an initial CE (ICE) of 85.24%. A capacity retention of 80.24% has been achieved after 500 stable cycles, with a high average CE of 99.64%. In contrast, the NVPOF full cell using NG4 electrolyte demonstrated an ICE of only 52.59%, and it failed after just ≈350 cycles, with an average CE of only 72.86%. These results are consistent with the Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> additive improved high-rate cycle stability in symmetric cells shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0002">2</a>, further suggesting that, the introduced additive could significantly improve the full cell's cycling and interfacial stability, while reducing the interfacial impedance and promoting the Na<sup>+</sup> ion transfer kinetics.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202414041-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202414041-sec-0070-title">2.5 CEI Characterizations and Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>To further explore the mechanism behind the excellent high-rate cycle stability of Na || NVP full cells, we characterized the surface morphology, structure, and chemical states of the cycled NVP cathode. The SEM image of the NVP surface after 100 cycles in AE100 electrolyte (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0005">5a</a>) reveals a relatively uniform morphology without evident cracks nor voids, which is in sharp contrast to the one after 100 cycles in NG4 electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0005">5b</a>), exhibiting pronounced wrinkles, cracks, and pores. High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) image in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0005">5c</a> shows that the NVP particle cycled in AE100 electrolyte is covered by a uniform CEI layer with a thickness of 2.0–2.5&nbsp;nm, which is about half the thickness of the CEI generated in NG4 electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0005">5d</a>). Such uniform and thin CEI is advantageous to reduce the interfacial impedance and promote the Na<sup>+</sup> ion kinetics across the interface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0036_R_d96921805e1542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Energy Environ. Sci. J. Electrochem. Soc.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414041-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/26e690b2-5c6e-4fbe-b7ef-3d43edf10a58/adfm202414041-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/26e690b2-5c6e-4fbe-b7ef-3d43edf10a58/adfm202414041-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e772e294-ee51-42a9-9be4-70035e485dfe/adfm202414041-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/26e690b2-5c6e-4fbe-b7ef-3d43edf10a58/adfm202414041-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414041-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414041" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The characterization of NVP cathode interface and the illustration of the EEI formation in pristine and modified electrolytes. Top-view SEM images of a NVP cathode after 100 cycles at 5C in a) AE100 electrolyte and b) NG4 electrolyte, c) and d) are their respective HRTEM images. e) XPS depth profiling of the Al 2p spectra of the CEI formed in AE100 electrolyte. f) Schematic diagram of the formation mechanism of the SEI and CEI with Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> added in the electrolyte.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>XPS depth profiling was further employed to analyze the compositional distribution in the CEI of the NVP surface after cycling in both electrolytes. According to the C 1s spectra shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a> (Supporting Information), both CEIs exhibit comparable peaks assigned to C─O, C─O, and C─C bonds,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0012_R_d96921805e1574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which should originate from the decomposition of the G4 solvent. Species derived from the decomposition of OTf<sup>−</sup> and G4 solvent, such as ─CF<i><sub>x</sub></i>, NaF, Na<sub>2</sub>O, and R─O─Na,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0039_R_d96921805e1587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Commun. Small">39</a>, <a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0045_R_d96921805e1587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Chem. Sci. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Energy Storage Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> are also detected from CEIs formed in both electrolytes (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>However, some peaks are only detectable from the CEI of the NVP cycled in AE100. For example, the peak located at 74.4&nbsp;eV in the Al 2p XPS spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0005">5e</a>) is assigned to Al─O bond,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0002_R_d96921805e1604" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Rev. Mater. Adv. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which is consistent with the Al─O peak at 530.1&nbsp;eV in the O 1s spectra<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0024_R_d96921805e1609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Commun.">24</a>, <a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0046_R_d96921805e1609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Science">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23a</a>, Supporting Information), indicating the existence of Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> in the CEI. Such Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-containing CEI can effectively promote the Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport kinetics<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0002_R_d96921805e1629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Rev. Mater. Adv. Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0016_R_d96921805e1629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Lett.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and enhance the NVP structural stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0024_R_d96921805e1634" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Commun.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the etching time increases (XPS probes deeper), the C content gradually decreases, and the Al content slightly increases in the CEI formed in AE100 electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30a</a>, Supporting Information), and thus the outer layer contains more organics, while inorganics (particularly Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>) are mainly distributed in the inner layer; the C content remains almost constant in the CEI formed in NG4 electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30b</a>, Supporting Information). Such CEI is more advantageous than that formed in NG4 to reduce the interfacial impedance and boost the Na<sup>+</sup> ion kinetics for enhanced durable high-rate cycle performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0002_R_d96921805e1652" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Rev. Mater. Adv. Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202414041-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414041-bib-0016_R_d96921805e1652" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Lett.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202414041-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202414041-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this work, we introduced the Al(EtO)<sub>3</sub> additive into a conventional ether-based Na electrolyte, to promote the simultaneous formation of a uniform, robust, highly Na<sup>+</sup> ion-conductive SEI on the Na metal anode, and a CEI on the NVP cathode. The strategy enables an ultra-stable cycling of Na symmetric cells for 4800&nbsp;h with a low voltage polarization of 283&nbsp;mV at 10&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, and achieves an excellent Na || NVP full cell performance, with a capacity retention of 81.28% after 6000 stable cycles at 40C. As illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414041-fig-0005">5f</a>, the SEI and CEI formed in the additive-containing electrolyte are rich in inorganics, with Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and Al metal uniformly distributed in the SEI, and Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> well-dispersed in the CEI; both are advantageous to facilitate the uniform and rapid Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport while promoting the rapid and homogeneous electrodeposition for the remarkably enhanced high-rate performance in Na metal batteries. Compared with conventional SMA stabilization strategies, the introduction of the organic aluminum additive here is a facile method to enhance the high-rate stability of both electrodes simultaneously, which may accelerate the development of long-lasting high-rate SMBs for practical applications.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
